<span id="freeTextContainer4138405130339226910">Clarke uses the classic A-B-A storytelling format for two different cities, A and B. A- ennui. B- learning!. A again- add learning to ennui equals stuff!! We see this often in literature. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23480032.Rude_Vile_Pigs' title='Rude Vile Pigs by Leo X. Robertson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rude Vile Pigs</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7870281.Leo_X__Robertson' title='Leo X. Robertson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Leo X. Robertson</a> is another shining example.<br/><br/>So good that I'll let him off with telling me his protagonist's feelings like EVERY TIME or ending chapters with stuff like "She just made a promise she couldn't keep", like, okay- are you telling me the twist in the coming chapters is that she doesn</span>
  <span id="freeText4138405130339226910" style="display:none">Clarke uses the classic A-B-A storytelling format for two different cities, A and B. A- ennui. B- learning!. A again- add learning to ennui equals stuff!! We see this often in literature. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23480032.Rude_Vile_Pigs" title="Rude Vile Pigs by Leo X. Robertson" rel="nofollow noopener">Rude Vile Pigs</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7870281.Leo_X__Robertson" title="Leo X. Robertson" rel="nofollow noopener">Leo X. Robertson</a> is another shining example.<br /><br />So good that I'll let him off with telling me his protagonist's feelings like EVERY TIME or ending chapters with stuff like "She just made a promise she couldn't keep", like, okay- are you telling me the twist in the coming chapters is that she doesn't keep the promise? Just twist it later, mate! Don't tell me you're gonna!<br /><br />They should really make this into a film. And I mean properly- they shouldn't steal from this and others and call it Interstellar. MUUUUURRRPPHHH (omg guys, this film is super serious. Take it super seriously. Like, all humanity seriously. So serious. Guys, don't joke about Interstellar. It's really, really, seriously serious. Seriously. Do you think that, like Looper, it was partially funded by the Chinese and that's why the end of humanity was, um "crop blight" (what the fuck) and not global warming? Okay clearly it was funded by someone. For the love of god, don't make the thing in your film everyone's going to see the actual pressing global issue! Don't educate them ffs- make it something lame, I beg you!!) Because later on some descriptions would be awe-inspiring on the big screen. Don't you think the latest sci-fi films have been fucking lazy? This book is from the 50s and has more ideas than 50+ Insterstellars and Ex Machinas. It's true that FILMS haven't done what those films did yet (or have they? I bet they have, even!), but the stories they told were told better in novels 60+ years old! Don't let them get away with it: they're not contributing anything to speculative fiction! I know I just asked for this novel to be made into a film, but then you're outright stating that this book was written when it was written- you're not claiming to have contributed anything new. It's just like all these comic book films too: their success is testament to just how many idiots don't fucking read. No such idiot is reading this text, though, so good for you. And hey! You! Don't you think there are so many fucking idiots about? I mean, Jesus!<br /><br />An aside (and not that Clarke did this here but) don't you hate it when novelists write films? JANUARY, 2006- THE WHITE HOUSE (uuummm this is a page. Not a screen. Just put me there you lazy hack bastard.) pew pew pew! They're shooting each other! Um, I don't care. These are just words. Do an emotion or two instead.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1085695508" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1085695508" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35299502-george"><img alt="George" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411721421p2/35299502.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1085695508?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/35299502-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35299502-george?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35299502-george?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1085695508" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12447498270494760816">Clarke does it again. In "The City & The Stars", he paints a vivid picture of humanity in the far future that has reached for the heavens before inevitably falling back to Earth and stagnating.<br/><br/>Enter our hero, who feels that there must be more to existence than the city he lives in and sets out to discover what else there is.<br/><br/>Much like "Rendezvous With Rama" there is no villain other than Man's ignorance and prejudice, and in truth this is a very gentle, if intriguing story.<br/><br/>So why do I think it is</span>
  <span id="freeText12447498270494760816" style="display:none">Clarke does it again. In "The City &amp; The Stars", he paints a vivid picture of humanity in the far future that has reached for the heavens before inevitably falling back to Earth and stagnating.<br /><br />Enter our hero, who feels that there must be more to existence than the city he lives in and sets out to discover what else there is.<br /><br />Much like "Rendezvous With Rama" there is no villain other than Man's ignorance and prejudice, and in truth this is a very gentle, if intriguing story.<br /><br />So why do I think it is so amazing? Simple - the vision. Clarke eloquently describes such technologies as 3d printing, wireless communication and energy transfer, genetic engineering, wormholes and wormhole-inducing-space engines, personal interactive holograms... the list goes on. Now you may quite reasonably ask "so what?". After all these are staples of sci-fi.<br /><br />This book was published in 1956. Most homes in the UK did not have television and nearly 50% did not have a landline.<br /><br />I like to think I have an original idea once in a while, but Clarke... he was a visionary. The things he could both conceive and express in layterms is breath-taking.<br /><br />Now all of the gushing aside, there are a couple of areas where the book is let down. The first is one of morality. Everyone that our hero encounters is very moral and virtuous. I have no problem with that, but there is a distinct lack of any faith, spirituality, or religion - that's fine as far as it goes, but then what has replaced that and how is then morality governed? It is not explained.<br /><br />I am told that "Beyond The Fall of Night" is a sort of sequel, and I will certainly put it on my "to-read" list. "City..." is complete, but there are a number of loose ends that I would love to see explored. What becomes of Mad Mind? What did call to the Galactic Empire and where are they now? What is They City's real mission?<br /><br />In conclusion, buy this, enjoy it, tell others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12447498270494760816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44737540-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601186771p2/44737540.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451079909" href="/book/show/149088.The_City_and_the_Stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44737540-patrick?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44737540-patrick?shelf=classics">classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2309212160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14279040585592273505">Classic 50s Sci-fi at its best! The City and the Stars is considered Clarke’s best novel and I agree. I loved this book! <br/><br/>The fact The City and the Stars takes place a billion years into the future completely grabbed my attention! What would be going on a billion years into the future? Well…nothing I expected! Earth is a desert wasteland except for the super technologically advanced city of Diaspar. The city was encased inside a protective dome, which kept the city cool and kept its citizens prot</span>
  <span id="freeText14279040585592273505" style="display:none">Classic 50s Sci-fi at its best! The City and the Stars is considered Clarke’s best novel and I agree. I loved this book! <br /><br />The fact The City and the Stars takes place a billion years into the future completely grabbed my attention! What would be going on a billion years into the future? Well…nothing I expected! Earth is a desert wasteland except for the super technologically advanced city of Diaspar. The city was encased inside a protective dome, which kept the city cool and kept its citizens protected from the outside elements—even from viewing the stars... The city was run by a mother-brain-like-entity called the Central Computer, which organized all human life in Diaspar. Humanity found immortality in something called memory banks. This reminded me of the vampires in Underworld who were frozen and took turns coming back. Everyone had a cycle in which they would eventually be awakened and begin life anew with the memories of their previous existence. <br /><br />Alvin, the main character, is a Unique. This means that he was the first human being born in over a few million years. Alvin’s “uniqueness” prompts him to seek life outside the city limits. <br /><br />This book was just as unique as Alvin and full of adventure. I love Clarke’s work but this book is his best in every way. I loved how the story progressed and how the city of Diaspar was set up. This was a great story and I really enjoyed it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14279040585592273505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1723179951" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1723179951" />
    <a title="Claudia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia"><img alt="Claudia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507465969p2/20498957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1723179951?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudia" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia">Claudia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/18739674-orasul-si-stelele">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=z-to-a-clarke">z-to-a-clarke</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1723179951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6494523736625653728">To be honest, I am a little disappointed. Mr. Clarke’s works usually are brimming with ideas, which here were not the case, unfortunately. It felt like a cartoon for children – the way characters are shaped, the environment, the robots, the city, the universe… <br/><br/>Maybe I did not get the message right; maybe this is how it was supposed to be – all the above to be just a blurred background for what the author wanted to transmit us: in isolation and without progress we regress and disappear but also t</span>
  <span id="freeText6494523736625653728" style="display:none">To be honest, I am a little disappointed. Mr. Clarke’s works usually are brimming with ideas, which here were not the case, unfortunately. It felt like a cartoon for children – the way characters are shaped, the environment, the robots, the city, the universe… <br /><br />Maybe I did not get the message right; maybe this is how it was supposed to be – all the above to be just a blurred background for what the author wanted to transmit us: in isolation and without progress we regress and disappear but also too much progress means losing identity. To summarize, conservatism is good but with progressive influences is way better.<br /><br />Well, the thing is the story left me cold… I did not get any emotion whatsoever reading it. Nor do I consider it wasted time. It was just a book which for me didn’t bring anything new.<br />-----<br /><u>Later edit about the lack of ideas</u>: one of my GR friends pointed out to me that <i>“it has, among many others, the first ever depiction of a virtual reality and this in 1948”</i> and he is right. Because this novel (appeared in 1956) is an extended version of a novella published in 1948. And yes, for those years it’s really visionary. Only on GR it was written that it was first published in 1987 and I thought that for those years didn’t seem to bring anything new.<br /><br />So, taken into consideration when it was written, yes, it does have a lot of innovative ideas. However, my feelings on it remain the same. I guess that’s because some books must be read at a certain point in life and especially in sci-fi, when recent ones are full of technological wonders, it is hard to be amazed now by a work written more than 50 years ago.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6494523736625653728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_721633483" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/721633483" />
    <a title="Carol Tensen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23576468-carol-tensen"><img alt="Carol Tensen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378053294p2/23576468.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/721633483?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol Tensen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol Tensen" href="/user/show/23576468-carol-tensen">Carol Tensen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23576468-carol-tensen?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer721633483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2969381738722248046">One of Arthur C. Clarke's earliest works, this is based on Against the Fall of Night. Oddly enough, he thought that it would be eclipsed by this book in the reading public's mind. They're both still in print. The events in the second half of this book unfolded too quickly to be really savored. All in all, it's still a very good read. Clarke's imagination never fails to delight.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review721633483">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_2960139207" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2960139207" />
    <a title="Stephane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76436730-stephane"><img alt="Stephane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563113233p2/76436730.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2960139207?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephane" href="/user/show/76436730-stephane">Stephane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781407244112" href="/book/show/22388041-the-city-and-the-stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76436730-stephane?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76436730-stephane?shelf=1950-59">1950-59</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76436730-stephane?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2960139207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17877194820837879026">The City and the Stars<br/>Arthur C. Clarke<br/><br/>It was not an easy task that Clarke set out to accomplish when he wrote <i>The City and the Stars</i>. To begin, the story takes place in the deep, deep, future (millions of year in the future.) This is, in itself, somewhat of a gamble; who can know what the world will be like then? Far future stories are almost always too familiar to be truly believable, and in some ways this is the case here, even when considering that the evolution of mankind has somewhat stoppe</span>
  <span id="freeText17877194820837879026" style="display:none">The City and the Stars<br />Arthur C. Clarke<br /><br />It was not an easy task that Clarke set out to accomplish when he wrote <i>The City and the Stars</i>. To begin, the story takes place in the deep, deep, future (millions of year in the future.) This is, in itself, somewhat of a gamble; who can know what the world will be like then? Far future stories are almost always too familiar to be truly believable, and in some ways this is the case here, even when considering that the evolution of mankind has somewhat stopped and stabilized for millions of years. It is just hard to believe that we will still be around in one billion years and that we will behave in a somewhat familiar way. <br /><br />On top of that, we are introduced to not one, but two visions of utopia. Utopias, really, are much harder to create than dystopias. It makes sense; what makes us miserable is quite universal, and what makes us truly happy is more likely to present individual variations. Ask people what there greatest fears or dissatisfactions are, and you are likely to be able to find some common ground. But ask them what makes them happy? You might get a different answer from everyone!<br /><br />So we should really pause to appreciate that Clarke managed to create not one, but two fairly credible utopias set in the deep future. The first one, Diaspar (despair? disappear? diaspora? the name begs for various interpretation...) is a technological utopia where death has been banished. Beautiful, super intelligent beings lead multiple lifes devoid of pain, need and illness, preoccupied mostly by artistic or profound intellectual pursuits. The second, Lys, is a more agrarian society evolving in close connection with a tame and benevolent nature, where even the wild beast have lost even the slightest glimmer of aggressively. More importantly, the development of telepathic power allows for genuine communal life and absolute true love between two beings. <br /><br />However for Alvin, the protagonist, the world is far from perfect. He is born on Diaspar, the technological utopia. He is living his first life, an extremely rare and mysterious event. His quest to find what is missing will be the core of the book. But what exactly is missing? Part of the answer is offered early on in the book: <br /><br /><i>It had never occurred to Alvin that Alystra was beautiful, for he had never seen human ugliness. When beauty is universal, it loses its power to move the heart, and only his absence can produce any emotional effect.</i> <br /><br />One could argue that everything good needs its opposite to exist, or at least the idea of it. In Diaspar even the idea of something deficient or imperfect has been banished, nothing bad existed, exist or will exist within the wall of the city. Since everything bad is gone, all there is left is the fear of losing this perfection, the fear of outside. This creates a stifled, stagnant society completely closed to the world, which is the source of Alvin’s despair. He needs to get out. <br /><br />The City and The Star turns out to be a pretty straightforward exploration and adventure story, and the characters are certainly forgettable, but the setting, this strange deep future utopia, the eerily vacant “ghost universe,” make it much more interesting that it would otherwise be. Clarke excels when he lays on the reader those “big ideas,” and I think that this is more evident here than perhaps any of his books. I don't know if it is the best book Clarke ever wrote, but it certainly deserves to be mentioned. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17877194820837879026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_774285791" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/774285791" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5903197-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311861358p2/5903197.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/774285791?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/5903197-tim">Tim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5903197-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer774285791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1549545151294163901">This is my second Clarke and it's way better than my first encounter with the man's work, which was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/140195.The_Space_Trilogy' title='The Space Trilogy by Arthur C. Clarke' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Space Trilogy</a>. Yes, I probably started with the wrong book, as my review indicates: see <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/982903034' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>. But hey, it was cheap and the stories weren't that long, so...<br/><br/>Anyway, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/250024.The_City_and_the_Stars' title='The City and the Stars by Arthur C. Clarke' rel='nofollow noopener'>The City and the Stars</a> (but the green-yellow cover of the reissue) was bought at the same time, though the reading was postponed, because of my bad experience with the other book. Unjustly, but it is what it is.<br/><br/>In this story, there </span>
  <span id="freeText1549545151294163901" style="display:none">This is my second Clarke and it's way better than my first encounter with the man's work, which was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/140195.The_Space_Trilogy" title="The Space Trilogy by Arthur C. Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener">The Space Trilogy</a>. Yes, I probably started with the wrong book, as my review indicates: see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/982903034" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. But hey, it was cheap and the stories weren't that long, so...<br /><br />Anyway, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/250024.The_City_and_the_Stars" title="The City and the Stars by Arthur C. Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener">The City and the Stars</a> (but the green-yellow cover of the reissue) was bought at the same time, though the reading was postponed, because of my bad experience with the other book. Unjustly, but it is what it is.<br /><br />In this story, there are two cities: Lys and Diaspar. Diaspar is covered by a dome and is technologically far more advanced than Lys, where mankind is still mankind. Natural births, telepathy (or communication via their minds, not as much in a spoken fashion), different kinds of people, etc. (Lys) vs no new births (but a sort of reincarnation, a reboot, if you will), oral communication, everyone with a perfect skin and look (no ugly people, so to speak) (Diaspar). And so on. There are more differences.<br /><br />Both cities don't know, or don't want to know (as later turns out), of each other's existence. Both consider the other inferior. Diaspar is controlled by a Central Computer, a Council governs the city, but relies on this Computer, on its memory banks, ... The city must not change, everything must remain according to the saved image in the Computer, even though the Computer has saved various images since the creation of the city. Like you would save an image of your hard-disk, so you don't have to re-install your software (operating system and more) from scratch.<br /><br />In Diaspar, you don't need to be able to cook or construct something. By a simple click of your fingers, you can conjure furniture, decorations, any food you'd like to eat (if the memory banks have information about it, of course, like recipes and how cooks used to prepare the food and meals back when Diaspar was not as advanced or not isolated under a dome), and so on.<br /><br />There is a new kid in town, however, named Alvin (a Unique, one who hasn't yet undergone rebirth), who wonders if there's a world outside of Diaspar and if (and how) it can be reached. Is there an exit (and entrance)? He decides to investigate, but somehow needs the help of one jester, Khedron. And yes, or what did you think?, there is life outside of Diaspar: Lys. There's even a direct, underground transport system, linea recta, which goes back and forth. Or at least, went, as there's a whole history attached to it.<br /><br />At some point in the future, Man was so advanced that it had moved into space to create an empire. But it is said that alien invaders pushed mankind back onto Earth. Man was so devastated by this event, by this defeat that its survivors decided to invest in progress on a smaller scale, hence the domed city and an underground transport-system (to protect themselves from those invaders). Until at some point, there was no need any more to use it, because Diaspar was perfect on its own. A closed circle of life, relying heavily on technology and computers.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Alvin discovers, with the help of Khedron, the transport-system, and even can travel with it. It still functions. That's when he arrives at Lys, in one of its villages, and establishes contact under false pretences (more or less). But when he wants to go back, they will have to erase his memories, because of the aforementioned trouble between Lys and Diaspar. Together with Hilvar, son of Seranis, who is the leader of the village, he explores the country and finds out more than expected. He has a plan to tell Diaspar of what's outside, but has to trick Seranis and the others to be able to flee without his memories erased. As I mentioned, the people of Lys have strong mental powers, can read one's mind easily (when permitted), communicate in a mental way (over small and large distances).<br /><br />Alvin also tricks Diaspar upon one of his few returns. In the end, both cities have no choice but to come together and re-establish contact and communication; be one people again. Also because Alvin and Hilvar have traveled into space, towards the worlds of the invaders of old (especially after having found a robot from that world, who had strict orders to not give away any crucial information about the Great Ones). One thing leads to another and thanks to the Central Computer, Alvin can use the robot for his own means, the robot follows his instructions. Alvin needs him, as he was the "driver" of the spaceship that was hidden in the desert between Diaspar and Lys, and knows the way to its home planet and the zone of the Seven Suns, an artificial construction in space. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Clarke described mainly events, actions, gave background information on the worlds, their behaviour, their beliefs and so on. The characters weren't all that well developed, I find. Was this characteristic for the period in which the book was written? Was it a deliberate decision? Yes, it's science-fiction (with a slab of fantasy), and characters aren't always the most important element in the writing story.<br /><br />What bothered me a lot, was that Hilvar and Alvin didn't seem to need to prepare for their journeys. Food? Clothing? Nature calls? (ok, a little too much unnecessary detail here, perhaps) None of that. Any other human being would have had a migraine, fainted because of malnourishment, fallen sick, ... As long as the story advances, who cares, right? Plus, some actions could happen a bit too smoothly, if you ask me. Or was that inherent to that kind of "perfect" civilisation?<br /><br />All in all, a very good story about the influence of religion, of how arrogance and so-called supremacy (no critical thinking here) - or power and (false) ambition - don't really help a civilisation move forward; it creates division and narrow-mindedness. Leaving it all in the hands of a central computer also isn't the answer, you're tying yourself, you're putting shackles on your arms and legs.<br /><br />A recommended read, one way or another, despite the flaws.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1549545151294163901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer11276148784881688117">3.5 stars - Arthur C. Clarke takes the reader on another epic journey.<br/><br/>This is the third Arthur C. Clarke book I've read (2001: a space odyssey, childhoods end) and once again he points out the insignificance of the human race in the grand scheme of things. 'The city and the stars' Is set in a future earth billions of years from now. There is only one city left on earth, Diaspar, which is preserved under a dome. Beyond the city of Diaspar is nothing, no oceans, no plant life, no life - just deser</span>
  <span id="freeText11276148784881688117" style="display:none">3.5 stars - Arthur C. Clarke takes the reader on another epic journey.<br /><br />This is the third Arthur C. Clarke book I've read (2001: a space odyssey, childhoods end) and once again he points out the insignificance of the human race in the grand scheme of things. 'The city and the stars' Is set in a future earth billions of years from now. There is only one city left on earth, Diaspar, which is preserved under a dome. Beyond the city of Diaspar is nothing, no oceans, no plant life, no life - just desert. The people of Diaspar are extremely evolved and have long since abolished crime, disease, birth and death achieving immortality through 'memory clouds' which preserve the individuals memories and self being, and is stored into the individuals brain in a body made and crafted by the powers of Diaspar to last hundreds of thousands of years. The cities residence are content with their uneventful easy way of life. The population of Diaspar have a built in fear of earths history and what's beyond their dome and have no intentions to leave, there is no way to leave Diaspar and even if the people could leave they would never dare to do so based on their fears. Then along comes Alvin, who is what they call a 'unique', a glitch in the system, and has a thirst for knowledge and the will to explore beyond the confines of Diaspar. Alvin can't be controlled by the forces of Diaspar that control its people, since he is a 'unique' and therefore does not share the same fears that are built into its people. Alvin does not fit in, and although he was 'born and raised' in Diaspar, he doesn't know how he came to be amongst these strange people and has no place in Diapers current way of life, He finds it wrong and questions everything. Alvin can't seem to make sense of the way of life which he finds unproductive and meaningless. Alvin believes that there is more out there and questions the history and beliefs of Diaspar's people and the higher powers that keep the order in the city. Alvin has an advantage being a 'unique' and uses that power to his advantage. Will Alvin find a way out of the dome to answer that burning Questions we all seem to have "how did we get here?" "What is the meaning of life?" "What else is there beyond earth" <br /><br />Another clever work by Arthur C. Clarke tackling the bigger picture in his own 'left of Center' way. He takes what we think we know, and in his signature style smashes common knowledge and belief diving deep into a world beyond comprehension unleashing the readers mind with his plausible views and concepts. Arthur C. Clarke atm is my favourite SF writer and once I finish my next book (life of Pi by Yann Martel) I'm going to read 'Rendezvous with Rama'. Looking forward to it</span>
  <a data-text-id="11276148784881688117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2411107561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15640271936079446613">Reviews and more <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://edwardsghostengine.wordpress.com/2018/08/28/the-city-and-the-stars-arthur-c-clarke/#more-6656'>https://edwardsghostengine.wordpress....</a><br/><br/>This was a very ambitious novel that I thought was very well researched for its time with this far future world believable to many including myself at first. It was just the pacing, unbelievable characters and far-fetched conclusion that kind of made me enjoy this less 😛<br/><br/>First I’ll talk about the good, I found I really got on with the writing style the author used here because it described everything in a very deep and philosophical way. By</span>
  <span id="freeText15640271936079446613" style="display:none">Reviews and more <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://edwardsghostengine.wordpress.com/2018/08/28/the-city-and-the-stars-arthur-c-clarke/#more-6656">https://edwardsghostengine.wordpress....</a><br /><br />This was a very ambitious novel that I thought was very well researched for its time with this far future world believable to many including myself at first. It was just the pacing, unbelievable characters and far-fetched conclusion that kind of made me enjoy this less 😛<br /><br />First I’ll talk about the good, I found I really got on with the writing style the author used here because it described everything in a very deep and philosophical way. By this I mean every time I read the description, the writing and narrative had a very epic legend-like feel to it that had me thinking in wonder more than once. The research the author must have done to add all these amazing details must have been tremendous and I can definitely give credit where it is due.<br /><br />Towards the end though I started struggling to finish this as I found the second half to be dragging, mostly due to not a lot happening and a lot of talk of doing things before actually doing them. The characters I felt had little to no depth to them as I never really cared about, well, anything about them 😛 The whole mystery surrounding the plot started off really intriguing but got boring towards the end as there was no build-up and the characters hardly ever got any closer to solving their problems. Instead everything was revealed and resolved in the last few pages leaving a lot of the book to be about planning and wandering.<br /><br />I won’t lie, I will admit I was a little disappointed with the outcome in the end as I found it a bit crude and far-fetched given that this is set billions of years into the future. I don’t feel there’s a great lot for me to say about this book as I’ve already said I really liked the writing style throughout but was disappointed with the way the plot went/moved along, the slow pace in the second half, the lack of proper character development and the conclusion that if I’m being really honest felt a bit rushed and forced, like the author had run out of ideas. 😛 But this is definitely a well crafted book, it gave me a genius glimpse of what might have been a possible future someday and also made me think on how easy it is to lose history when we rely on machines to do everything for us… Still a good book with plenty of ideas and ambition, just could have flowed better and been more exciting in my opinion.<br /><br /><br />Who I’d recommend this for<br /><br />Science fiction fans who are looking for something different, love good writing and enjoy classics. Be warned though, this is not exactly an exciting adventure like a lot of Sci-Fi today, it relies more on words and language to move the story along.</span>

<div id="review_1733822294" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1733822294" />
    <a title="Ken Doggett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26895953-ken-doggett"><img alt="Ken Doggett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475147230p2/26895953.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1733822294?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken Doggett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken Doggett" href="/user/show/26895953-ken-doggett">Ken Doggett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780151180233" href="/book/show/940087.The_City_And_The_Stars">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1733822294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10558873582735674109">From the time I was a teenager and all the way through young adulthood Arthur C. Clarke was my favorite writer. I would read anything of his I could find, including non-fiction, because I found it to be highly entertaining and enjoyable. Like many books of his, I thought I had already read this one. As far as I can now determine, I hadn't. Or maybe it was so long ago that I don't remember it.<br/><br/>The book came out in 1956, when I was about 10 years old, and before I was a Clarke fan. The copy I have </span>
  <span id="freeText10558873582735674109" style="display:none">From the time I was a teenager and all the way through young adulthood Arthur C. Clarke was my favorite writer. I would read anything of his I could find, including non-fiction, because I found it to be highly entertaining and enjoyable. Like many books of his, I thought I had already read this one. As far as I can now determine, I hadn't. Or maybe it was so long ago that I don't remember it.<br /><br />The book came out in 1956, when I was about 10 years old, and before I was a Clarke fan. The copy I have came from the library, and it appears to be one of the 1956 originals; the bottom edge of the spine is worn from sliding across many shelves, and the top of the spine is frayed from many fingers reaching into it to pull the book out. It has a forward by Clarke explaining why he rewrote "Against the Fall of Night" and renamed it "The City and the Stars." He also explains that much of this story was presented in that earlier work.<br /><br />I found the first half of the book somewhat boring as Clarke goes through his world building and character introductions, and I got the impression that this was the part that came from the earlier work. As I moved into the second half of the story, the writing style and pace seemed to improve, and although I found none of it believable, I enjoyed the sense of wonder and of the vastness of time and space. Clarke was the creator of Big Ideas, and his readers came to expect that.<br /><br />As with most Science Fiction of that time, characterization is on the shallow side, and you never feel as if you are a part of the scene instead of just watching it. Some readers will see this as a fault of the writer, but Clarke knew his audience of mostly young men, and knew how to write for them. I confess that at the time, I never noticed the shallowness of his characters, and would probably have seen detailed characterization as getting in the way of the story. I <i>liked</i> the Big Idea, and wanted to get right into it.<br /><br />My tastes in fiction have greatly changed. If I had never heard of Clarke, and read this today as the only example I had seen of his work, I would probably not read anything else of his. However, he wrote for his audience of the time, and I and a lot of other readers derived much enjoyment from reading his work. True to form, this story takes you from a city on Earth in the far future, back into the past, and into the far reaches of space, exploring all the way, introducing you to robots, spaceships, and unknown worlds. In the end, I found that I still enjoyed his exploration of the Big Idea. I rate it at 4 out of 5 stars, and recommend it to any young reader who wants to explore a classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10558873582735674109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_215354044" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/215354044" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/215354044?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552083829" href="/book/show/940088.City_And_The_Stars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>

That strange man didn't let me down, I really enjoyed this classic piece of science fiction from the Grandmaster. It's a science fiction adventure the likes of which they just can't s</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer619602498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14556248334122754115">Set billions of years into the future of humanity, this novel raises a lot more questions than it answers about our destiny. This might, though, have been the point. The characters are less personable than is usual in Clarke's novels, which did mar my enjoyment a little, but otherwise The City and the Stars is an intriguing and thought-provoking look at the role of mankind in space.<br/><br/>Read as part of my Arthur C. Clarke 2013 Reading Challenge. Review to follow shortly at <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://forwinternights.wordpress.com/'>http://forwinternights.word</a></span>
  <span id="freeText14556248334122754115" style="display:none">Set billions of years into the future of humanity, this novel raises a lot more questions than it answers about our destiny. This might, though, have been the point. The characters are less personable than is usual in Clarke's novels, which did mar my enjoyment a little, but otherwise The City and the Stars is an intriguing and thought-provoking look at the role of mankind in space.<br /><br />Read as part of my Arthur C. //<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Fj/irLbmqhchO9KmU8irTY21xnP5Y488bnwBDeh6gLnEZt1UJGzoAdoLAh2p74IrQcElesIQ8j5g5PQfdQWqmw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JI0zTYO3vrvNyBF6wshlWPwjWhV5mZG5pcKkx7Wv0MD21Ay1ET38rTb4wcE470w+MFe5HELq7LurWlHVKND64g==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7779.Arthur_C_Clarke">About Arthur C. Clarke</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7779.Arthur_C_Clarke"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1357191481p3/7779.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7779.Arthur_C_Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
8,240
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15715875800234998499">Arthur Charles Clarke was one of the most important and influential figures in 20th century science fiction. He spent the first half of his life in England, where he served in World War Two as a radar operator, before emigrating to Ceylon in 1956. He is best known for the novel and movie <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i>, which he co-created with the assistance of Stanley Kubrick.<br/><br/>Clarke was a graduate of King</span>
  <span id="freeText15715875800234998499" style="display:none">Arthur Charles Clarke was one of the most important and influential figures in 20th century science fiction. He spent the first half of his life in England, where he served in World War Two as a radar operator, before emigrating to Ceylon in 1956. He is best known for the novel and movie <i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i>, which he co-created with the assistance of Stanley Kubrick.<br /><br />Clarke was a graduate of King's College, London where he obtained First Class Honours in Physics and Mathematics. He is past Chairman of the British Interplanetary Society, a member of the Academy of Astronautics, the Royal Astronomical Society, and many other scientific organizations.<br /><br />Author of over fifty books, his numerous awards include the 1961 Kalinga Prize, the AAAS-Westinghouse science writing prize, the Bradford Washburn Award, and the John W. Campbell Award for his novel <i>Rendezvous With Rama</i>. Clarke also won the Nebula Award of the Science Fiction Writers of America in 1972, 1974 and 1979, the Hugo Award of the World Science Fiction Convention in 1974 and 1980, and in 1986 became Grand Master of the Science Fiction Writers of America. He was awarded the CBE in 1989.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15715875800234998499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
